B00AYR2FOI|4.0|I think of &#34;Memphis&#34; as the third part of a sort of trilogy that begin with Boz Scaggs' &#34;Some Change&#34; (1994) and &#34;Come on Home&#34; (1997).  This is music for grownups, informed by soul and the blues, with a natural, rootsy, earthy appeal -- good songs played by mature musicians and sung by an experienced vocalist who knows how to convey what a song is actually about.  Much credit to producer/drummer Steve Jordan for his efforts, and you can't be too surprised that sessions featuring Ray Parker, Jr. (look him up -- he played on a lot of things before he did &#34;Ghostbusters&#34;), Willie Weeks and Spooner Oldham, among others, sound so good.<br /><br />A highlight for me is &#34;Gone Baby Gone,&#34; an Al Green-style tune written by Scaggs that establishes just the right tone for this collection of songs (&#34;album,&#34; for those too young to remember or care).  I'm a big fan of Willy DeVille, and Scaggs' engaging version of &#34;Mixed Up, Shook Up Girl&#34; casts some deserving light on the work of the late performer and song writer.  And while I happen to like DeVille's menacing interpretation of &#34;Cadillac Walk&#34; more, Scaggs' more rollicking take of the Moon Martin song is another highlight here.  The gentle take on &#34;Corrina, Corrina&#34; is a change of pace that might quietly insinuate itself into your brain and/or heart.<br /><br />Think of the title &#34;Memphis&#34; as a signpost that this is more Royal Studios in Memphis than &#34;Silk Degrees&#34; in the 1970s ... although fans of that album and &#34;70s Boz&#34; can like this a lot if they're willing to give Scaggs and his team a chance.  New fans are welcome, as well, and should find a lot to like here.<br /><br />This is music of and for the long haul.  &#34;Memphis&#34; will grow on you, if you let it.

My one small criticism (and it is a small one) is that there was very little variation in the tracks across both albums. With her third release, The Absence, she has addressed this and produced her greatest album.<br /><br />Teaming up with Heitor Pereira, she has moved away from the smoky blues/chanteuse style of her earlier albums to embrace a more Latin feel. She is now using a slightly richer backing, tinged with Latin rhythms. She breathes her way through delicate and delightful tracks that speak of love, life and loss. It's slow, but with an infectious beat. And then suddenly, unexpectedly we get `Goodbye' where she picks up the pace and puts a little more power into her voice to give us something different and unexpected. B00BNWWUDY|4.0|From the opening shot--which duplicates the opening camerawork of PSYCHO--SIDE EFFECTS plays out like a by-the-numbers tribute to Alfred Hitchcock.  We have the strong female protagonist and a befuddled but determined male lead; duplicitous supporting characters; and a third-act twist that, if you know your Hitchcock, you can see coming, but is still a nice revelation nonetheless.<br /><br />The plot is predictably complicated: Emily's (Rooney Mara) husband is finally getting released from jail, where he's been doing time for some white collar crimes (take that, Wall Street!).  When Martin (Channing Tatum) gets out, Emily, instead of being overjoyed, drives her car into a wall.  Chance--or is it?--leads her to psychiatrist Jonathan Banks (Jude Law), who gives her a new medicine he's been encouraged to prescribe.  Things get better initially--Emily's sex drive is given a jump start, for one--but then turn very sour very quickly.  (I shan't give away more, although the trailers more or less spoiled certain plot elements.)<br /><br />Steven Soderbergh has his legions of fans.  Personally, I've never been blown away by his work; he has his good films (OCEAN'S 11, TRAFFIC) and his stinkers (OCEANS 12 and 13).  SIDE EFFECTS is the first of his films I've seen that doesn't really feel like a Soderbergh film, probably because so much emphasis is upon paying Hitchcock his due homage.  The plot rolls along at a decent pace, and there's a nice change of focus about halfway through, where the film shifts from Mara's shoulders to Law's.  Both actors give riveting performances; the same can be said of the supporting cast, especially Catherine Zeta-Jones as Emily's past psychiatrist.  (Yes, even Tatum continues this career upswing he's been on, proving that he's actually a good actor, and not just eye candy for the ladies.)  The script is solid; none of the twists are really mind-blowing, as perhaps they're meant to be, but the dialogue is decent, upheld by the caliber of performances.<br /><br />If anything, this is SIDE EFFECTS' main weakness: it doesn't have much to offer on its own.  Sure, the acting is solid, and there's some nice social commentary (about twenty years too late, but what the heck), but the film is basically a reworking of the basic Hitchcock tropes.  So basically, if you know your film history, you might not be able to get too far into this one.  However, if you're a determined Soderbergh fan (the man's been over-hyped, but there's no denying he has talent), or if you are unfamiliar with the Master of Suspense, you my find something new in SIDE EFFECTS.  Otherwise, the movie is a solid tribute, but perhaps could have been more than the sum of its parts.nces.<br /><br />If anything, this is SIDE EFFECTS' main weakness: it doesn't have much to offer on its own.
